First, peopleâ€™s understandings of abuse have shifted a lot over time, and they continue to evolve based on the work of advocacy and support groups, changes in laws and legal systems, new research and scholarship, cultural movements, advances in the fields of psychology and trauma treatment, and lots more. Second, the representations of abuse that we most commonly see can be misleading. For instance, itâ€™s easy to show a picture of someone with visible physical injuries, but a lot of abuse is far more subtle and harder to represent in a photo. News articles and TV clips about abuse often focus primarily on situations that involve extreme and frequent acts of physical violence, because those are the types of abuse that are most likely to come to the attention of a reporter or writer.   • Am I never really sure they are telling the truth or giving me the whole story? Do I leave conversations with them feeling confused or disoriented?

  • Are they malicious or mean-spirited toward me? Do they set me up to fail, or mock me or humiliate me?

  • Do they play mind games with meâ€”twisting my words to mean things they donâ€™t, reinterpreting events to cast me in a bad light, accusing me of things I havenâ€™t done, or otherwise bending reality to justify being upset at me?

æ€è€ƒçˆ±çš„å®šä¹‰å¦‚ä½•ä¸æ„å»ºå‡¯è’‚Â·æ–¯ç‰¹èµ–å…‹æ‰€ç§°çš„â€œçŸ¥æƒ…åŒæ„æ–‡åŒ–â€(consent culture) çš„æ•´ä½“é¡¹ç›®ç›¸è”ç³»ï¼Œè¿™ä¹Ÿå¯èƒ½æ˜¯æœ‰ä»·å€¼çš„ã€‚å¯¹å¥¹æ¥è¯´ï¼ŒçŸ¥æƒ…åŒæ„æ–‡åŒ–æ˜¯ç›®æ ‡ï¼Œå®ƒæ˜¯å¼ºå¥¸æ–‡åŒ–çš„å¯¹ç«‹é¢ï¼Œè€Œå¼ºå¥¸æ–‡åŒ–æ˜¯å…è®¸è™å¾…çŒ–ç—çš„èƒŒæ™¯ã€‚åœ¨å¥¹çš„çŸ¥æƒ…åŒæ„æ–‡åŒ–ç»ƒä¹ å†Œã€Šé—®ä½ è‡ªå·±ã€‹ä¸­ï¼Œæ–¯ç‰¹èµ–å…‹å†™é“ï¼šâ€œæˆ‘æƒ³é¼“åŠ±5 äººä»¬æ‰¹åˆ¤æ€§åœ°æ€è€ƒè‡ªå·±çš„ç”Ÿæ´»å’Œç»å†ï¼Œæ€è€ƒä»–ä»¬åœ¨å“ªé‡Œå¯ä»¥é€‰æ‹©çŸ¥æƒ…åŒæ„ï¼Œè€Œä¸æ˜¯æˆ‘ä»¬ç¤¾ä¼šæ‰€é¼“åŠ±çš„æƒåˆ©æ„Ÿã€è§£ç¦»å’Œå°†è‡ªå·±æ¨å‘æé™çš„ä»·å€¼è§‚ã€‚â€

In a way, this formulation acts as a society-level counterpart to the question â€œIs this relationship loving?â€ But it also comes right back down to interpersonal relationships. Does a loving relationship include non-negotiated entitlement, in the sense of assuming you can do things to your partners without asking and receiving a clear yes, or that you can disregard your effects on your partners? Does it require you to dissociate in order to get through what youâ€™re doing together? Does it require you to push yourself beyond your limits? Weâ€™d say no to all these questions: They donâ€™t fit into bell hooksâ€™s understanding of love. And if you understand yourself as a person who wants to build a consent culture, what better place to start than with your own relationships?

Definitions of abuseâ€‹

è™å¾…çš„å®šä¹‰â€‹

The term abuse is commonly used to refer to two main concepts: coercive control and toxic behaviours. These concepts are both worth explaining. They arenâ€™t one and the same, but they do overlap. This is important because the slippage between them can make some discussions (and even your internal thought processes) confusing.

æœ¯è¯­â€œè™å¾…â€é€šå¸¸ç”¨æ¥æŒ‡ä»£ä¸¤ä¸ªä¸»è¦æ¦‚å¿µï¼šå¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶å’Œæœ‰æ¯’è¡Œä¸ºã€‚è¿™ä¸¤ä¸ªæ¦‚å¿µéƒ½å€¼å¾—è§£é‡Šã€‚å®ƒä»¬å¹¶ä¸å®Œå…¨ç›¸åŒï¼Œä½†ç¡®å®æœ‰é‡å ã€‚è¿™å¾ˆé‡è¦ï¼Œå› ä¸ºå®ƒä»¬ä¹‹é—´çš„æ··æ·†ä¼šä½¿ä¸€äº›è®¨è®ºï¼ˆç”šè‡³ä½ çš„å†…éƒ¨æ€ç»´è¿‡ç¨‹ï¼‰å˜å¾—æ··ä¹±ã€‚

COERCIVE CONTROL å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶

Some people use the term abuse when theyâ€™re talking about coercive control and related harms as part of an ongoing pattern. In a report to Canadaâ€™s House of Commons,6 scholar Carmen Gill defined coercive control as follows: â€œCoercive control encompasses acts of both coercion and control through the use of force, deprivation, humiliation, intimidation, exploitation, isolation, and domination. This is done to produce a victimâ€™s obedience, ultimately eliminating their sense of freedom in the relationship.â€ In The Globe and Mail,7 Gill elaborates, referring to partners who engage in power games â€¦ itâ€™s gaslighting, lies, blaming, cruelty, intimidationâ€”all those things that we donâ€™t necessarily recognize as a form of violence [under] the Criminal Code of Canada. (â€¦) Itâ€™s isolation from friends and family. Restricted access to money or food or medicine. Damaging property, or hurting pets. Degrading comments. Barrages of text messages. Monitoring social media.

æœ‰äº›äººåœ¨è°ˆè®ºä½œä¸ºæŒç»­æ¨¡å¼ä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†çš„å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶å’Œç›¸å…³ä¼¤å®³æ—¶ä½¿ç”¨â€œè™å¾…â€ä¸€è¯ã€‚åœ¨æäº¤ç»™åŠ æ‹¿å¤§ä¸‹è®®é™¢çš„ä¸€ä»½æŠ¥å‘Šä¸­ï¼Œ6 å­¦è€…å¡é—¨Â·å‰å°” (Carmen Gill) å°†å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶å®šä¹‰å¦‚ä¸‹ï¼šâ€œå¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶åŒ…æ‹¬é€šè¿‡ä½¿ç”¨æ­¦åŠ›ã€å‰¥å¤ºã€ç¾è¾±ã€æå“ã€å‰¥å‰Šã€å­¤ç«‹å’Œæ”¯é…æ¥è¿›è¡Œçš„å¼ºåˆ¶å’Œæ§åˆ¶è¡Œä¸ºã€‚è¿™æ ·åšæ˜¯ä¸ºäº†è®©å—å®³è€…æœä»ï¼Œæœ€ç»ˆæ¶ˆé™¤ä»–ä»¬åœ¨å…³ç³»ä¸­çš„è‡ªç”±æ„Ÿã€‚â€åœ¨ã€Šç¯çƒé‚®æŠ¥ã€‹(The Globe and Mail)7 ä¸­ï¼Œå‰å°”è¯¦ç»†é˜è¿°äº†é‚£äº›å‚ä¸å…¶ä¸­çš„ä¼´ä¾£ æƒåŠ›æ¸¸æˆâ€¦â€¦å®ƒæ˜¯ç…¤æ°”ç¯æ•ˆåº” (gaslighting)ã€è°è¨€ã€æŒ‡è´£ã€æ®‹å¿ã€æå“â€”â€”æ‰€æœ‰é‚£äº›æˆ‘ä»¬ä¸ä¸€å®šæ ¹æ®ã€ŠåŠ æ‹¿å¤§åˆ‘æ³•ã€‹æ‰¿è®¤ä¸ºæš´åŠ›å½¢å¼çš„äº‹æƒ…ã€‚ï¼ˆâ€¦â€¦ï¼‰å®ƒæ˜¯ä¸æœ‹å‹å’Œå®¶äººçš„éš”ç¦»ã€‚é™åˆ¶è·å–é‡‘é’±ã€é£Ÿç‰©æˆ–è¯ç‰©ã€‚ç ´åè´¢äº§ï¼Œæˆ–ä¼¤å®³å® ç‰©ã€‚è´¬ä½çš„è¯„è®ºã€‚çŸ­ä¿¡è½°ç‚¸ã€‚ç›‘æ§ç¤¾äº¤åª’ä½“ã€‚

Coercive control may or may not come with direct physical violence; it is abuse regardless. But a pattern of physical violence almost always occurs within a context of coercive control. The important thing here is to recognize that coercive control encompasses emotional and psychological abuse, as well as other kinds, such as financial abuse and stalking, and that all of these are real forms of abuse whether or not they include physical or sexual assault. Patterns of coercive control may also extend to control over another personâ€™s perceptions of reality itselfâ€”insisting that abusive incidents never happened or were the other personâ€™s fault, for example, or trying to convince someone they are mentally unwell in order to get them to accept another personâ€™s control over their decisions. This is, of course, what we call gaslighting, and it is both an abuse tactic and a form of abuse in itself.

å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶å¯èƒ½ä¼´éšä¹Ÿå¯èƒ½ä¸ä¼´éšç›´æ¥çš„èº«ä½“æš´åŠ›ï¼›æ— è®ºå¦‚ä½•å®ƒéƒ½æ˜¯è™å¾…ã€‚ä½†èº«ä½“æš´åŠ›çš„æ¨¡å¼å‡ ä¹æ€»æ˜¯å‘ç”Ÿåœ¨å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶çš„èƒŒæ™¯ä¸‹ã€‚è¿™é‡Œé‡è¦çš„æ˜¯è¦è®¤è¯†åˆ°ï¼Œå¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶åŒ…æ‹¬æƒ…æ„Ÿå’Œå¿ƒç†è™å¾…ï¼Œä»¥åŠå…¶ä»–ç±»å‹ï¼Œå¦‚ç»æµè™å¾…å’Œè·Ÿè¸ªï¼Œæ‰€æœ‰è¿™äº›éƒ½æ˜¯çœŸæ­£çš„è™å¾…å½¢å¼ï¼Œæ— è®ºå®ƒä»¬æ˜¯å¦åŒ…æ‹¬èº«ä½“æˆ–æ€§ä¾µçŠ¯ã€‚å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶çš„æ¨¡å¼ä¹Ÿå¯èƒ½å»¶ä¼¸åˆ°æ§åˆ¶å¦ä¸€ä¸ªäººå¯¹ç°å®æœ¬èº«çš„æ„ŸçŸ¥â€”â€”ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼ŒåšæŒè®¤ä¸ºè™å¾…äº‹ä»¶ä»æœªå‘ç”Ÿè¿‡æˆ–è€…æ˜¯å¦ä¸€ä¸ªäººçš„é”™ï¼Œæˆ–è€…è¯•å›¾è®©æŸäººç›¸ä¿¡ä»–ä»¬ç²¾ç¥ä¸æ­£å¸¸ï¼Œä»¥ä¾¿è®©ä»–ä»¬æ¥å—å¦ä¸€ä¸ªäººå¯¹å…¶å†³å®šçš„æ§åˆ¶ã€‚è¿™å½“ç„¶å°±æ˜¯æˆ‘ä»¬æ‰€è¯´çš„ç…¤æ°”ç¯æ•ˆåº”ï¼Œå®ƒæ—¢æ˜¯ä¸€ç§è™å¾…ç­–ç•¥ï¼Œä¹Ÿæ˜¯ä¸€ç§è™å¾…å½¢å¼æœ¬èº«ã€‚

The nature of coercive control is that one person is deliberately skewing the power dynamic to instill fear and obedience in their partner. Itâ€™s very rare that a person exerting coercive control will relinquish their power willingly; they are systematically undermining their partnerâ€™s ability to self-advocate. This is generally the kind of dynamic weâ€™re talking about in this chapter when we use the term abuse.

å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶çš„æœ¬è´¨æ˜¯ä¸€ä¸ªäººæ•…æ„æ­ªæ›²æƒåŠ›åŠ¨æ€ï¼Œå‘å…¶ä¼´ä¾£çŒè¾“ææƒ§å’Œæœä»ã€‚å®æ–½å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶çš„äººå¾ˆå°‘ä¼šè‡ªæ„¿æ”¾å¼ƒæƒåŠ›ï¼›ä»–ä»¬åœ¨ç³»ç»Ÿåœ°ç ´åä¼´ä¾£è‡ªæˆ‘è¾©æŠ¤çš„èƒ½åŠ›ã€‚è¿™é€šå¸¸æ˜¯æˆ‘ä»¬åœ¨æœ¬ç« ä¸­ä½¿ç”¨â€œè™å¾…â€ä¸€è¯æ—¶æ‰€æŒ‡çš„é‚£ç§åŠ¨æ€ã€‚

For the sake of safety, rather than thinking about ways to fix the relationship, the person on the receiving end of this kind of harm might want to invest in building an escape plan: storing important documents at a friendâ€™s house, saving up money and choosing a strategic moment to move out while the abusive partner is away, for example. People experiencing coercive control are often in the greatest danger of being assaulted or even murdered right after they escape, as the abuser attempts to regain control by any means necessary.8 People experiencing abuse often know this to be true, which is why they often donâ€™t leave until theyâ€™re prepared and readyâ€”which can be difficult to achieve when theyâ€™re being coercively controlled. (This is one of the many reasons â€œWhy didnâ€™t they just leave?â€ is such an ignorant question!) Weâ€™ll discuss these dynamics further and provide resources later in this chapter.

ä¸ºäº†å®‰å…¨èµ·è§ï¼Œä¸å…¶è€ƒè™‘ä¿®å¤å…³ç³»çš„æ–¹æ³•ï¼Œæ‰¿å—è¿™ç§ä¼¤å®³çš„äººå¯èƒ½æƒ³è¦æŠ•èµ„å»ºç«‹ä¸€ä¸ªé€ƒè·‘è®¡åˆ’ï¼šä¾‹å¦‚ï¼ŒæŠŠé‡è¦æ–‡ä»¶å­˜æ”¾åœ¨æœ‹å‹å®¶ï¼Œæ”’é’±ï¼Œé€‰æ‹©ä¸€ä¸ªæ–½è™ä¼´ä¾£ä¸åœ¨çš„æˆ˜ç•¥æ—¶åˆ»æ¬å‡ºå»ã€‚é­å—å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶çš„äººå¾€å¾€åœ¨é€ƒè·‘åç«‹å³é¢ä¸´è¢«è¢­å‡»ç”šè‡³è¢«è°‹æ€çš„æœ€å¤§å±é™©ï¼Œå› ä¸ºæ–½è™è€…è¯•å›¾ä¸æƒœä¸€åˆ‡ä»£ä»·é‡æ–°è·å¾—æ§åˆ¶æƒã€‚8 é­å—è™å¾…çš„äººé€šå¸¸çŸ¥é“è¿™æ˜¯çœŸçš„ï¼Œè¿™å°±æ˜¯ä¸ºä»€ä¹ˆä»–ä»¬ç»å¸¸åœ¨å‡†å¤‡å¥½ä¹‹å‰ä¸ä¼šç¦»å¼€â€”â€”è€Œåœ¨å—åˆ°å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶æ—¶ï¼Œè¿™å¯èƒ½å¾ˆéš¾å®ç°ã€‚ï¼ˆè¿™ä¹Ÿæ˜¯ä¸ºä»€ä¹ˆâ€œä»–ä»¬ä¸ºä»€ä¹ˆä¸ç›´æ¥ç¦»å¼€ï¼Ÿâ€æ˜¯ä¸€ä¸ªå¦‚æ­¤æ— çŸ¥çš„é—®é¢˜çš„ä¼—å¤šåŸå› ä¹‹ä¸€ï¼ï¼‰æˆ‘ä»¬å°†åœ¨æœ¬ç« ç¨åè¿›ä¸€æ­¥è®¨è®ºè¿™äº›åŠ¨æ€å¹¶æä¾›èµ„æºã€‚

TOXIC BEHAVIOUR æœ‰æ¯’è¡Œä¸º

The second main way people use the term abuse is when referring to toxic and harmful behaviour more generally. People may exhibit toxic behaviour of many kinds, up to and including physical violence, outside a context of coercive control. For example, a person might make a belittling comment to their partner or lie to them about something. They may engage in emotional blackmail, like guilt-tripping a partner or threatening to end a relationship if they donâ€™t get their way. Itâ€™s even pretty common to engage in gaslighting, like when someone feels ashamed of something they did and tries to minimize it, even when they know how bad it was. And while that behaviour may be anything from unpleasant to a deal-breaker, if itâ€™s not part of a pattern of nonconsensual domination enforced against another person, it wouldnâ€™t be considered coercive control. Similarly, if a person assaults someone on the street, thatâ€™s a criminal act, but itâ€™s not coercive control, because itâ€™s not part of an ongoing pattern of intimidation within a relationship.

äººä»¬ä½¿ç”¨è™å¾…ä¸€è¯çš„ç¬¬äºŒç§ä¸»è¦æ–¹å¼æ˜¯æŒ‡æ›´æ™®éçš„æœ‰æ¯’å’Œæœ‰å®³è¡Œä¸ºã€‚äººä»¬å¯èƒ½ä¼šè¡¨ç°å‡ºå¤šç§æœ‰æ¯’è¡Œä¸ºï¼Œç”šè‡³åŒ…æ‹¬èº«ä½“æš´åŠ›ï¼Œä½†è¿™å¹¶ä¸åœ¨å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶çš„èƒŒæ™¯ä¸‹ã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œä¸€ä¸ªäººå¯èƒ½ä¼šå¯¹ä¼´ä¾£å‘è¡¨è´¬ä½æ€§çš„è¯„è®ºæˆ–åœ¨æŸäº‹ä¸Šå¯¹ä»–ä»¬æ’’è°ã€‚ä»–ä»¬å¯èƒ½ä¼šè¿›è¡Œæƒ…æ„Ÿå‹’ç´¢ï¼Œæ¯”å¦‚è®©ä¼´ä¾£æ„Ÿåˆ°å†…ç–šï¼Œæˆ–è€…å¦‚æœä»–ä»¬æ²¡æœ‰è¾¾åˆ°ç›®çš„å°±å¨èƒç»“æŸå…³ç³»ã€‚ç”šè‡³ç…¤æ°”ç¯æ•ˆåº”ä¹Ÿç›¸å½“æ™®éï¼Œæ¯”å¦‚å½“æŸäººä¸ºè‡ªå·±æ‰€åšçš„äº‹æƒ…æ„Ÿåˆ°ç¾è€»å¹¶è¯•å›¾å°†å…¶æœ€å°åŒ–æ—¶ï¼Œå³ä½¿ä»–ä»¬çŸ¥é“é‚£æœ‰å¤šç³Ÿç³•ã€‚è™½ç„¶è¿™ç§è¡Œä¸ºå¯èƒ½ä»ä»¤äººä¸å¿«åˆ°æˆä¸ºç ´åå…³ç³»çš„å› ç´ ï¼Œä½†å¦‚æœå®ƒä¸æ˜¯é’ˆå¯¹å¦ä¸€ä¸ªäººå®æ–½çš„éè‡ªæ„¿æ”¯é…æ¨¡å¼çš„ä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†ï¼Œå®ƒå°±ä¸ä¼šè¢«è§†ä¸ºå¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶ã€‚åŒæ ·ï¼Œå¦‚æœä¸€ä¸ªäººåœ¨è¡—ä¸Šè¢­å‡»æŸäººï¼Œé‚£æ˜¯çŠ¯ç½ªè¡Œä¸ºï¼Œä½†ä¸æ˜¯å¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶ï¼Œå› ä¸ºå®ƒä¸æ˜¯å…³ç³»ä¸­æŒç»­æå“æ¨¡å¼çš„ä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†ã€‚

When someone displays toxic behaviour, it may be possible for the people involved to work through it, if the person displaying the behaviour is willing to change and the person or people receiving it are willing to forgive. For example, if someone insults others, lies or cheats, while it might cause deep hurt, they might be able to resolve the problem through conversation or therapy, and it might never reoccur. Despite the instance or instances of toxic behaviour, the power dynamic between the people involved might remain relatively egalitarian. And it might be possible to repair the relationship rupture or breakdown caused by the harm. The strategies you might want to employ to resolve this kind of situation could include clear boundary-setting; communication exercises; individual, joint or family therapy; targeted skill-building or anger management courses; and so on. The fate of the relationship depends on each personâ€™s decisions about whether to stay or leave, to change or remain entrenched.

å½“æœ‰äººè¡¨ç°å‡ºæœ‰æ¯’è¡Œä¸ºæ—¶ï¼Œå¦‚æœè¡¨ç°å‡ºè¿™ç§è¡Œä¸ºçš„äººæ„¿æ„æ”¹å˜ï¼Œè€Œæ¥å—è¿™ç§è¡Œä¸ºçš„äººæ„¿æ„åŸè°…ï¼Œé‚£ä¹ˆç›¸å…³äººå‘˜å¯èƒ½ä¼šè§£å†³è¿™ä¸ªé—®é¢˜ã€‚ä¾‹å¦‚ï¼Œå¦‚æœæœ‰äººä¾®è¾±ä»–äººã€æ’’è°æˆ–æ¬ºéª—ï¼Œè™½ç„¶è¿™å¯èƒ½ä¼šé€ æˆæ·±æ·±çš„ä¼¤å®³ï¼Œä½†ä»–ä»¬å¯èƒ½èƒ½å¤Ÿé€šè¿‡å¯¹è¯æˆ–æ²»ç–—æ¥è§£å†³é—®é¢˜ï¼Œå¹¶ä¸”å¯èƒ½æ°¸è¿œä¸ä¼šå†å‘ç”Ÿã€‚å°½ç®¡å‘ç”Ÿäº†ä¸€æ¬¡æˆ–å¤šæ¬¡æœ‰æ¯’è¡Œä¸ºï¼Œç›¸å…³äººå‘˜ä¹‹é—´çš„æƒåŠ›åŠ¨æ€å¯èƒ½ä»ç„¶ç›¸å¯¹å¹³ç­‰ã€‚ä¿®å¤ç”±ä¼¤å®³é€ æˆçš„å…³ç³»ç ´è£‚æˆ–å´©æºƒå¯èƒ½æ˜¯å¯èƒ½çš„ã€‚ä½ å¯èƒ½æƒ³è¦ç”¨æ¥è§£å†³è¿™ç§æƒ…å†µçš„ç­–ç•¥åŒ…æ‹¬æ˜ç¡®çš„ç•Œé™è®¾å®šï¼›æ²Ÿé€šç»ƒä¹ ï¼›ä¸ªäººã€è”åˆæˆ–å®¶åº­æ²»ç–—ï¼›æœ‰é’ˆå¯¹æ€§çš„æŠ€èƒ½åŸ¹å…»æˆ–æ„¤æ€’ç®¡ç†è¯¾ç¨‹ï¼›ç­‰ç­‰ã€‚å…³ç³»çš„å‘½è¿å–å†³äºæ¯ä¸ªäººå…³äºæ˜¯ç•™ä¸‹è¿˜æ˜¯ç¦»å¼€ã€æ”¹å˜è¿˜æ˜¯å›ºå®ˆçš„å†³å®šã€‚

Itâ€™s also entirely common for people to engage in toxic behaviours toward each other. This could be because neither of them has ever learned healthy relating, one could be reacting to the other, or they could be setting each other off in particularly explosive ways. Some people call these relationships â€œmutually abusive,â€ but we agree with domestic violence prevention organizations that such a thing doesnâ€™t exist, because abuse (as coercive control) always involves a one-sided power dynamic. People being mean to each other can be awful, and certainly counts as toxic behaviour, but because itâ€™s mutual, itâ€™s not coercive control. Finally, itâ€™s also common for victims of coercive control to use toxic behaviours in self-defence, or to adopt some of the tactics of their abusers as survival strategies.9

Some toxic behaviours might be instant deal-breakersâ€”actions that make you decide to end a relationship right away, before they can possibly escalate to a pattern. For example, if someone hits you once, you might decide to leave immediately rather than stick around to give them a second chance or an opportunity to repair things. Or if someone makes an insult that crosses a major line for you, and you feel it was deliberate and maliciousâ€”or it simply hurts you so much you donâ€™t feel you can trust them againâ€”that might be a relationship-ender: They donâ€™t need to repeat it for it to be sufficient grounds for you to leave. This is completely reasonable. Nobody should feel they have to tolerate or forgive harmful acts that cross their boundaries, even if they happen only once. It is totally valid to say, â€œI donâ€™t want to be with a person (or in a polycule) who could or would ever do that,â€ and end things on the spot. You do not owe anyone a second chance once they have harmed you.

Putting them together ç»¼åˆæ¥çœ‹

Neither coercive control nor toxic behaviour are acceptable in any kind of relationship, intimate or otherwise. Even in the absence of coercive control, toxic behaviour can certainly be considered abusive, and one or two instances of toxic behaviour might in fact be the starting point for a coercive control dynamic. Such behaviours may be the red flags or warning signs of the beginnings of a pattern of harm. A pattern always starts somewhere, after all; abuse rarely shows up all in one shot. It escalates once a person is already feeling attached and has something to lose by leaving.

If youâ€™re experiencing toxic behaviour from a partner, but it hasnâ€™t crossed a clear line for you, this is where your sense of discernment becomes extremely valuable. Difficult times happen in every relationship; over time, youâ€™ll always discover things you donâ€™t like about your partners, or have experiences where youâ€™d like them to have behaved better. But remember, a lot of abusers get away with their abuse by making their target question their own perceptions or experiences. And unfortunately, some abusers are very skilled manipulators who are invested in making sure their victims remain under their spell. Are you giving them the benefit of the doubt and being compassionate, or are you being manipulated into letting someone cross your boundaries? It can be hard to tell.

Many domestic violence researchers refer to situational and characterological violence. Daniel Joseph Friend et al. explain10 that situational violence is â€œmutual, low-level violence (i.e., pushing or grabbing) perpetrated by both partners as a means of conflict management,â€ whereas characterological violence is when a person uses violence to induce fear in their victim in order to manipulate and control them. The researchers specify that the psychological abuse in situational violence is â€œsimilar to the psychological abuse seen in characterological violence, but it occurs less frequently and is absent of controlling and dominating behaviors.â€ (This concept maps onto the distinction weâ€™ve drawn between coercive control and toxic behaviour.)

The word â€œcharacterologicalâ€ refers to a problem with a personâ€™s character, meaning personality. In the psychological literature, this idea describes a personâ€™s long-term, often lifelong, pattern of thinking and behaviour: a tendency to see themselves as the victim, to believe that violence is often legitimate, to feel entitled to certain kinds of deference, and so on. For our purposes, the idea here is not to make a connection between characterological violence and personality per se, and weâ€™re not here to diagnose people with disorders (or suggest that you try to, either). The important idea is the notion of a repeated pattern over timeâ€”in this case, a pattern of doing harm to partners and otherwise being â€œbad actors,â€ meaning people who consistently return to the same problematic and harmful behaviours across numerous situations and partnerships.

We contend that characterologically violent people include many of the folks we call missing stairs. The blogger Cliff Pervocracy coined the term a missing stair to describe a dangerous person within a community whom everybody has just gotten used to working around or warning each other aboutâ€”like a missing stair in a staircaseâ€”instead of setting clear boundaries or enacting direct consequences for the personâ€™s bad behaviour.11 If youâ€™re part of a nonmonogamous, kink or sex-positive community, you may have encountered this phenomenon, or maybe even â€œtripped on a missing stairâ€ yourself. This scenario isnâ€™t inherent to nonmonogamy itself, but it may become relevant if you get involved in community groups. Itâ€™s not in any way exclusive to sex-and relationship-related communities, eitherâ€”you might know a missing stair in your workplace, at your gym or in any number of other settings. In addition, sometimes a missing stair is in fact a community leader of some kind, or otherwise in a powerful position, with the ability to ostracize or otherwise punish anyone who speaks out. Arguably, this kind of situation might exceed the usual definition of a missing stair. Warning signs of abuseâ€‹

è™å¾…çš„è­¦ç¤ºä¿¡å·â€‹

The following are some signs of a potentially abusive relationship or polycule, especially if they happen persistently over time:

ä»¥ä¸‹æ˜¯æ½œåœ¨è™å¾…å…³ç³»æˆ–å¤šè¾¹å…³ç³»ç½‘ç»œçš„ä¸€äº›è¿¹è±¡ï¼Œç‰¹åˆ«æ˜¯å¦‚æœå®ƒä»¬éšç€æ—¶é—´çš„æ¨ç§»æŒç»­å‘ç”Ÿï¼š

  • Your partner controls who you talk with or see and where you go.

  • Your partner controls your access to money.

  • Your partner destroys your property.

  • Your partner threatens you with violence or behaves in a physically intimidating way.

  • You feel afraid of your partner.

  • You feel frequently demeaned or humiliated by a partner or metamour.

  • You feel pressured or forced to engage in sex or kink that you donâ€™t want.

  • You feel that acceptance by your polycule depends on your participation in group sex.

  • A partner or metamour reads your messages, emails, journals or other private information without your permission, or wants to make a rule that these must be shared.

  • A partner or metamour threatens to harm you, your children or pets, or themselves if you leave them.

  • You find yourself doubting your own grip on reality, especially as it pertains to a relationship.

  • You feel like a partner or metamour is â€œtwo different people,â€ or like you never know whether they will hurt you or support you in any given moment.

  • You feel discouraged from communicating with your metamours.

  • You feel you are expected to keep secrets from or about your metamours.

  • Youâ€™re made to feel that you are â€œnot really nonmonogamousâ€ if you express a concern, ask for a limit or communicate your feelings.

  • You feel shamed for seeking out social supports outside your relationship or polycule.

  • A partner or metamour invalidates your feelings or internal experience.

  • A partner or metamour claims to be a gatekeeper, or the only or best source of reliable information about nonmonogamy.

  • You feel that no one else will want to be with you or â€œput up with youâ€ if you leave.

  • You feel like the sole problem in a relationship or polycule.

The best resource for people in nonmonogamous relationships that might be harming them is The Network/La Red, or TNLR (tnlr.org). Itâ€™s a survivor-led organization in the United States that specifically addresses partner abuse among queer, kinky and nonmonogamous people, so you can ask for help without having to go through the whole â€œwhy the nonmonogamy isnâ€™t the problem hereâ€ routine. [cite_start]A sort of inversion can also happen:[cite: 1323] Mononormative values can be used to shame you so that you believe, at some level, that you are causing harm simply by being nonmonogamous, and you need to compensate somehow for your very presence in a partnerâ€™s life. It can be easy to use the structures of nonmonogamy to enact harm. For example, in a triad, if two people agree on something and the third person doesnâ€™t, those two people need to take great care to avoid ganging up on the outlier and pressuring them to agree to something they donâ€™t want just because theyâ€™re outnumbered. Also, a hinge or pivot partner in a vee or larger structure can blame everything thatâ€™s going wrong on the partner whoâ€™s not in the room at the time, and thus avoid taking responsibility for their own choices.

When a whole polycule gets involved, though, things can get particularly bad. [cite_start]This is where some cult theory[cite: 1323] can be useful. In cults, resources are funnelled inward from rank-and-file members toward a central figure, the cult leader. The leader keeps a close cadre of intimates around them, but instills a sense of instability among the members of this inner circle to keep them off-balance, and thus trying to please. They may move up or down in rank, or fall in or out of favour. All of this instability keeps the leader in control. In a polycule, this kind of manipulation can manifest as â€œgood partner/bad partnerâ€ dynamicsâ€”with bonus points if everyone thinks they are the good partner and someone else is the bad partner! [cite_start]This tracks with the dynamic that Lundy Bancroft discusses in Why Does He Do That? in which an abuserâ€™s new partner becomes their biggest ally[cite: 1324] against the previous, abused partnerâ€”only here, itâ€™s translated into concurrent relationships.

In the less immediate term, you will need to heal. That may look very different depending on your particular experience, your needs and the support you have available. You may also need to make various practical decisions. For instance, you may need to pursue legal measures such as filing for divorce and navigating child custody, or engage in processes such as selling a home or breaking a lease. You may need to make decisions about disclosure: Do you want to disclose the abuse publicly, to select people, or only confidentially? Also, do you want to pursue legal recourse of any kind, undertake community justice or restorative justice measures, or simply move on? Make sure that whatever you choose to do is based on your own needs and goals, and not on pressure from others to take the measures they think are appropriate. You know you best. Every situation is unique. There is no one right way to do any of these things, no perfect prescription, and no required timeline.

Just as experiencing an abusive monogamous relationship can change your relationships going forward, experiencing abusive nonmonogamy can change the way you engage in nonmonogamous relationships. You might decide you donâ€™t want to be nonmonogamous at all anymore, or that you want to take a break from it (many people take a pause from relationships entirely after an abusive oneâ€”itâ€™s up to you). You might also find that you have new challenges that you didnâ€™t have before. For example, maybe you had never experienced jealousy, but exposure to abusive triangulation now makes you hypervigilant about your partnersâ€™ other relationships. Or you may have once been inclined toward kitchen table polyamory (see chapter 19), but now you can only engage in strictly parallel relationships. You may get triggered by anything that feels like a lack of transparency, or on the flip side, like your privacy has been violated. You might panic at the idea of conflict, even if you were once someone who communicated with ease and confidence.

Thereâ€™s also a related idea that people with strong partners cannot be abusive; nothing could be further from the truth. If abuse has happened to you, it wasnâ€™t your fault. Avoiding future abuse doesnâ€™t mean snuffing out these qualities, but learning to recognize dangerous and exploitative people early on and swiftly removing them from your life.

If you think you might be abusing someoneâ€‹

å¦‚æœä½ è®¤ä¸ºä½ å¯èƒ½æ­£åœ¨è™å¾…æŸäººâ€‹

When you read the descriptions of coercive control and toxic behaviour earlier, or the lists of red flags, did you recognize any of your own behaviour?

å½“ä½ é˜…è¯»å‰é¢å…³äºå¼ºåˆ¶æ§åˆ¶å’Œæœ‰æ¯’è¡Œä¸ºçš„æè¿°ï¼Œæˆ–è€…å±é™©ä¿¡å·åˆ—è¡¨æ—¶ï¼Œä½ æ˜¯å¦è®¤å‡ºäº†ä½ è‡ªå·±çš„ä»»ä½•è¡Œä¸ºï¼Ÿ

Maybe you learned how to be abusive because you were raised in an abusive environment. Maybe youâ€™re terrified of being powerless or out of control, and so you control your partners or try to maintain the upper hand as a way of managing this fear. Maybe you have a hard time being emotionally vulnerable, so you shield yourself from that by lashing out or treating others with contempt. Maybe you lack empathy and just donâ€™t really care whether you harm othersâ€”which may or may not be part of a personality disorder or a mental illness. Maybe youâ€™re dealing with addiction, and when youâ€™re drunk or high you have a hard time controlling violent or angry impulses. Maybe youâ€™re highly sensitive to criticism or are shame-bound, and you gaslight your partners because it hurts too much to be held accountable when you cause harm.

ä¹Ÿè®¸ä½ å­¦ä¼šäº†è™å¾…ï¼Œæ˜¯å› ä¸ºä½ åœ¨ä¸€ä¸ªè™å¾…ç¯å¢ƒä¸­é•¿å¤§ã€‚ä¹Ÿè®¸ä½ å®³æ€•æ— åŠ›æˆ–å¤±æ§ï¼Œæ‰€ä»¥ä½ æ§åˆ¶ä½ çš„ä¼´ä¾£æˆ–è¯•å›¾ä¿æŒä¸Šé£ï¼Œä»¥æ­¤æ¥ç®¡ç†è¿™ç§ææƒ§ã€‚ä¹Ÿè®¸ä½ å¾ˆéš¾åœ¨æƒ…æ„Ÿä¸Šè„†å¼±ï¼Œæ‰€ä»¥ä½ é€šè¿‡çŒ›çƒˆæŠ¨å‡»æˆ–è”‘è§†ä»–äººæ¥ä¿æŠ¤è‡ªå·±å…å—è¿™ç§è„†å¼±ã€‚ä¹Ÿè®¸ä½ ç¼ºä¹åŒç†å¿ƒï¼ŒçœŸçš„ä¸åœ¨ä¹æ˜¯å¦ä¼¤å®³ä»–äººâ€”â€”è¿™å¯èƒ½æ˜¯ä¹Ÿå¯èƒ½ä¸æ˜¯äººæ ¼éšœç¢æˆ–ç²¾ç¥ç–¾ç—…çš„ä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†ã€‚ä¹Ÿè®¸ä½ æ­£åœ¨å¤„ç†æˆç˜¾é—®é¢˜ï¼Œå½“ä½ é†‰é…’æˆ–å…´å¥‹æ—¶ï¼Œä½ å¾ˆéš¾æ§åˆ¶æš´åŠ›æˆ–æ„¤æ€’çš„å†²åŠ¨ã€‚ä¹Ÿè®¸ä½ å¯¹æ‰¹è¯„é«˜åº¦æ•æ„Ÿæˆ–å—ç¾è€»æ„ŸæŸç¼šï¼Œä½ å¯¹ä¼´ä¾£è¿›è¡Œç…¤æ°”ç¯æ•ˆåº”æ˜¯å› ä¸ºå½“ä½ é€ æˆä¼¤å®³æ—¶è¢«è¿½ç©¶è´£ä»»å¤ªç—›è‹¦äº†ã€‚

We hope you want to stop, and if you do, we hope you take action. To get there, you need to stop rationalizing and excusing your behaviour, or blaming it on others. Depending on how bad things have gotten, you might need to remove yourself from the situation so that you stop posing a danger to the people around youâ€”which might mean leaving a relationship temporarily or permanently. If youâ€™re struggling with addiction, you need to get sober. And you need to get professional help to find your way out of this situation, heal your own wounds, manage your emotions (especially anger and fear), learn better communication, handle any mental illness or substance problems youâ€™re dealing with, and become a better person. This might mean a recovery program, therapy, medication, support groups, anger management classes, or any number of other strategies depending on your situation.

æˆ‘ä»¬å¸Œæœ›ä½ æƒ³åœæ­¢ï¼Œå¦‚æœä½ æƒ³åœæ­¢ï¼Œæˆ‘ä»¬å¸Œæœ›ä½ é‡‡å–è¡ŒåŠ¨ã€‚ä¸ºäº†åšåˆ°è¿™ä¸€ç‚¹ï¼Œä½ éœ€è¦åœæ­¢ä¸ºä½ çš„è¡Œä¸ºå¯»æ‰¾ç†ç”±å’Œå€Ÿå£ï¼Œæˆ–è€…å°†å…¶å½’å’äºä»–äººã€‚å–å†³äºäº‹æƒ…å˜å¾—å¤šç³Ÿç³•ï¼Œä½ å¯èƒ½éœ€è¦è®©è‡ªå·±è„±ç¦»è¿™ç§æƒ…å†µï¼Œè¿™æ ·ä½ å°±ä¸å†å¯¹å‘¨å›´çš„äººæ„æˆå±é™©â€”â€”è¿™å¯èƒ½æ„å‘³ç€æš‚æ—¶æˆ–æ°¸ä¹…åœ°ç¦»å¼€ä¸€æ®µå…³ç³»ã€‚å¦‚æœä½ æ­£åœ¨ä¸æˆç˜¾ä½œæ–—äº‰ï¼Œä½ éœ€è¦æ¸…é†’è¿‡æ¥ã€‚ä½ éœ€è¦è·å¾—ä¸“ä¸šå¸®åŠ©æ¥æ‘†è„±è¿™ç§å±€é¢ï¼Œæ²»æ„ˆè‡ªå·±çš„åˆ›ä¼¤ï¼Œç®¡ç†æƒ…ç»ªï¼ˆå°¤å…¶æ˜¯æ„¤æ€’å’Œææƒ§ï¼‰ï¼Œå­¦ä¹ æ›´å¥½çš„æ²Ÿé€šï¼Œå¤„ç†ä½ æ­£åœ¨é¢å¯¹çš„ä»»ä½•ç²¾ç¥ç–¾ç—…æˆ–è¯ç‰©é—®é¢˜ï¼Œå¹¶æˆä¸ºä¸€ä¸ªæ›´å¥½çš„äººã€‚è¿™å¯èƒ½æ„å‘³ç€åº·å¤è®¡åˆ’ã€æ²»ç–—ã€è¯ç‰©ã€æ”¯æŒå°ç»„ã€æ„¤æ€’ç®¡ç†è¯¾ç¨‹ï¼Œæˆ–è€…æ˜¯å–å†³äºä½ æƒ…å†µçš„ä»»ä½•å…¶ä»–ç­–ç•¥ã€‚

Recognize, too, that on top of harming people close to you, your abusive actions may have terrible consequences for you, up to and including life-altering legal repercussions. You can stop this behaviour before external forces do it for you in ways that might be pretty awful. It is possible to change. The responsibility is yours. You can stop, but you need to decide to, and you need to make concrete changes. Start now.

On the use and misuse of adviceâ€‹

å…³äºå»ºè®®çš„ä½¿ç”¨å’Œè¯¯ç”¨â€‹

Remember how we mentioned earlier that any system or tool can be twisted and misused to cause harm? Thatâ€™s true for relationship advice in general, and for this chapter in particular. Itâ€™s not hard to picture a scenario where someone reads this book or this chapter and twists up our words to do harm. â€œRemember that time you got angry at me? Thatâ€™s abuse! We canâ€™t stop this from happening (which, honestly, is pretty distressing), but we can say a couple of things in the hope of mitigating it. First, this book is intended for you to use to better understand yourself and think through what you want, need and value in the realm of nonmonogamy and relationships, and to help you make decisions that bring you toward integrity, kindness and, ideally, joy. Itâ€™s not meant as a set of standards with which to shame or bludgeon others, or for others to use this way against you.

Second, pay attention to how you react to feeling hurt or harmed within your relationships. If, when you feel hurt or distressed, you find yourself reacting by trying to control your partnersâ€™ actions or force them to do what you want, punishing them for their failures, taking revenge, making fresh rules, or feeling better and safer when theyâ€™re off-balance or under your thumb in some way, that may be an indication that you are becoming abusive. A healthy relationship is not adversarial, with someone winning and someone losing. If you feel good when someone else feels bad, that means you have an emotional incentive to harm peopleâ€”and thatâ€™s a problem.

å…¶æ¬¡ï¼Œæ³¨æ„ä½ åœ¨å…³ç³»ä¸­æ„Ÿåˆ°å—ä¼¤æˆ–å—å®³æ—¶çš„ååº”ã€‚å¦‚æœå½“ä½ æ„Ÿåˆ°å—ä¼¤æˆ–ç—›è‹¦æ—¶ï¼Œä½ å‘ç°è‡ªå·±çš„ååº”æ˜¯è¯•å›¾æ§åˆ¶ä¼´ä¾£çš„è¡Œä¸ºæˆ–å¼ºè¿«ä»–ä»¬åšä½ æƒ³åšçš„äº‹ï¼Œæƒ©ç½šä»–ä»¬çš„å¤±è´¥ï¼ŒæŠ¥å¤ï¼Œåˆ¶å®šæ–°è§„åˆ™ï¼Œæˆ–è€…å½“ä»–ä»¬å¤„äºå¤±è¡¡çŠ¶æ€æˆ–ä»¥æŸç§æ–¹å¼åœ¨ä½ çš„æŒæ§ä¹‹ä¸‹æ—¶æ„Ÿåˆ°æ›´å¥½å’Œæ›´å®‰å…¨ï¼Œè¿™å¯èƒ½è¡¨æ˜ä½ æ­£åœ¨å˜å¾—å…·æœ‰è™å¾…æ€§ã€‚å¥åº·çš„å…³ç³»ä¸æ˜¯å¯¹æŠ—æ€§çš„ï¼Œä¸æ˜¯æœ‰äººèµ¢æœ‰äººè¾“ã€‚å¦‚æœä½ åœ¨åˆ«äººæ„Ÿè§‰ç³Ÿç³•æ—¶æ„Ÿè§‰è‰¯å¥½ï¼Œé‚£æ„å‘³ç€ä½ æœ‰ä¼¤å®³ä»–äººçš„æƒ…æ„ŸåŠ¨æœºâ€”â€”è¿™å°±æ˜¯ä¸ªé—®é¢˜ã€‚

In contrast, if your hurt or distress pushes you to try and fix or solve things, examine and correct your own behaviour, set clearer boundaries, ask for what you need, empathize with the other personâ€™s point of view and listen to their side of the story, collaborate, and ultimately seek harmony and peace, chances are that youâ€™re not an abuserâ€”emotional chaos is not rewarding to you. To the extent that youâ€™re willing and able, we hope you use this book and this chapter as a tool for self-reflection to help you think through your own situation, but not as a tool to try and prove that youâ€™re right, push someone else to change, or make them feel bad about themselves. From this point onward, everything in this book is written from the assumption that abuse is not present in the relationships, unless otherwise mentioned.

QUESTIONS TO ASK YOURSELFâ€‹

This chapter is already filled with questions and checklists, so weâ€™ll keep this section short. Here are a few final questions to reflect on:

æœ¬ç« å·²ç»å……æ»¡äº†é—®é¢˜å’Œæ£€æŸ¥æ¸…å•ï¼Œæ‰€ä»¥æˆ‘ä»¬å°†ä¿æŒè¿™ä¸€éƒ¨åˆ†çš„ç®€çŸ­ã€‚ä»¥ä¸‹æ˜¯æœ€åå‡ ä¸ªéœ€è¦åæ€çš„é—®é¢˜ï¼š

  • Are my partners or my polycule my sole sources of support? What other resources do I have available to me?

  • Are there patterns of behaviour I have witnessed or experienced, in my intimate relationships or my family of origin, that I want to learn to leave behind? Do I need to seek help for this work, and if so, where can I seek it?

  • What does accountability look like to me, for myself and for other people? Does my approach ultimately lead to healthier and more resilient relationships?

  • To whom would I turn for support if I were being harmed in my relationships or polycule? To whom would I turn for support if someone told me I had done harm?17
